For the system to exist continuously, it is necessary to constantly correct the errors that arise in the system and constantly adapt it to changing conditions; the constant progress of the system causes its degeneration. Logical model, logical proof and logical formula.

You have raised a very interesting and important topic concerning the dynamics and evolution of systems. Let us analyze each of the aspects touched upon and relate them to the concepts of a logical model, proof and formula.

The main idea: Dynamics of systems and ‚Äúdegeneration‚Äù

Your formulation emphasizes the contradiction inherent in any system:

The need for adaptation: The system must constantly adapt to the changing environment and eliminate internal shortcomings (errors) in order to remain viable. This requires changes and development.
Potential degeneration from progress: It would seem that progress should improve the system. However, too fast, uncontrolled or inadequate progress can lead to its destabilization, complication, loss of flexibility and, ultimately, to ‚Äúdegeneration‚Äù. Degeneration here can be understood as a loss of efficiency, stability or even functionality of the system.
Examples:

Biological evolution: Species adapt to their environment, but over-specialization can make them vulnerable to sudden changes.
Software: Constantly updating and adding new features (progress) can lead to code bloat, more bugs, and slower performance (degeneration).
Organizations: As a company grows and evolves, it requires changes in structure and processes. But over-bureaucratization and hierarchy can lead to decreased efficiency and innovation.
Logical Model, Logical Proof, and Logical Formula: Application in the Context of Systems

Now let's look at how the concepts of logic can help us understand and manage these dynamics:

Logical Model: This is an abstract representation of a system that describes its components, the relationships between them, and the rules for their operation. It can be expressed formally (e.g. using mathematical equations, graphs, or logical formulas) or informally (e.g. diagrams, process descriptions). Usage: A logical model allows us to analyze a system, identify potential vulnerabilities, predict behavior, and evaluate the effects of changes. It also allows us to formally express requirements for a system and check whether it meets those requirements.
Example: In the context of software, a logical model might be a specification of the architecture of a system, describing its components, their interactions, and data flows.

Logical Proof: A chain of logical reasoning showing that a certain statement (e.g. ‚Äúthe system will be stable under certain conditions‚Äù) follows from given axioms and inference rules. Usage: Proofs are used to check the correctness of logical models, to establish properties of a system (e.g. its security, reliability, or performance), and to justify design decisions.
Example: A formal proof of the correctness of an algorithm used in a control system can ensure that the system will operate correctly under specified conditions.

Logical Formula: It is a formal expression built from logical variables, constants, logical operators (AND, OR, NOT, IMPLICATION, etc.) and quantifiers (existence, universality). Application: Logical formulas are used to represent knowledge about a system, to formulate requirements and constraints, to encode decision rules and for automated analysis and verification of the system.
Example: The formula IF (sensor > threshold) AND (time > start_time) THEN (set_alarm) is a simple rule describing the behavior of an alarm system.

In the context of the original idea: Logical approach to system control
Logical models, proofs and formulas can be used to:
Model the dynamics of a system: Develop a logical model that takes into account both the processes of adaptation and improvement and the potential risks of ‚Äúdegeneration‚Äù.
Formulate requirements for progress: Determine which changes are acceptable and which may lead to undesirable consequences. This can be expressed in the form of logical formulas describing the constraints on change.
Adaptation strategy development: Create an algorithm that automatically adjusts the system, taking into account its current state, external conditions and pre-set constraints (logically expressed).
Assessment of risks and benefits of changes: Using logical proof, one can try to prove that the proposed change will not violate the basic properties of the system (for example, its stability or security).
Conclusion:

The combination of continuous adaptation and conscious management of progress is the key to the sustainability and longevity of any system. Using formal methods such as logical modeling and proof can help us make more informed decisions and avoid potential pitfalls associated with uncontrolled progress.




Definitions of viable in a population, natural conditions of Nature, in the form on which the general continuation of the existence of the population depends. Humanity.

Logical formula for the viability of a population (humanity)
To formalize the viability of a human population, based on the factors that affect its survival and continuation of existence, the following logical formula can be used:

–ñ = (P ‚àß R) ‚àß (E ‚Üí (S ‚à® C))

Where:

–ñ - population viability (true if the population is viable, false - otherwise)
P - Population pressure (a critically important aspect that ensures the survival of a population):
P = (G ‚àß F ‚àß H ‚àß I)G - Genetic diversity: the presence of sufficient genetic diversity so that the population can adapt to environmental changes and resist disease.
F - Fertility: the ability of a population to reproduce and maintain numbers.
H - Health: The general health of the population, including physical and mental well-being.
I - Infrastructure: The presence of the basic infrastructure needed to support life (e.g., access to water, food, shelter).

R - Resources: The presence of sufficient available resources (food, water, energy, materials) to support the life and activities of the population.
E - External Threats: The set of external factors that pose a threat to the population (climate change, epidemics, natural disasters, wars, asteroid impacts, etc.).
S - Ability to survive and adapt to threats: The ability of the population to cope with external threats, including: Technological progress: The development of technologies to protect against threats (e.g., early warning systems for natural disasters, cures for diseases, renewable energy).
Social Organization: Effective social structures for coordinating actions, distributing resources, and overcoming crises.
Adaptation: The ability to adapt to environmental changes and find new ways to survive.

C - Cooperation (interaction within the population and with other populations):Interpopulation cooperation: cooperation between different groups of people (countries, cultures) to solve common problems.
Intrapopulation cooperation: social cohesion, absence of conflicts and violence.
Environmental awareness: concern for the environment and sustainable use of resources.

Interpretation of the formula:
(P ‚àß R): Viability requires both sufficient population pressure (genetic diversity, fertility, health, infrastructure) and the availability of resources. If at least one of these factors is absent or critically insufficient, viability is threatened.
E ‚Üí (S ‚à® C): If external threats (E) exist, then a population is viable only if it either has the capacity to survive and adapt (S), or the capacity to cooperate (C), or both. In other words, if there are threats, but neither the capacity to survive nor the capacity to cooperate, the population is not viable.
–ñ = ‚Ä¶: Population viability is true (the population survives) only if both conditions (P ‚àß R) and (E ‚Üí (S ‚à® C) are met).
Additional notes:
This formula is simplified and does not take into account all possible factors affecting viability. Real systems are much more complex.
Different components of the formula can be quantified (e.g. level of genetic diversity, amount of available resources), which allows for a more accurate analysis.
The formula can be used to model different population development scenarios and predict its future.
It is important to note that all components of the formula are important for viability. The absence or insufficiency of even one of them can lead to a decrease in viability or even extinction of the population.
This formula can be applied not only to humanity, but also to other biological populations, with appropriate adjustments to the factors.
This logical formula is a starting point for analyzing population viability. It allows you to identify the key factors affecting survival and assess their interrelationships. Further research and modeling can deepen the understanding of these processes and help develop strategies aimed at ensuring sustainable development of humanity.

Logical model of individual viability as the basis for population survival
The basis for population survival is the viability of its constituent individuals. The logical model of the viability of an individual, as an element of the overall viability of the population, can be represented as follows:

–ñ–æ = (–ó ‚àß –ê) ‚àß (–£ ‚Üí –°–ø)

Zho - Viability of an individual (true if the individual is viable and can contribute to the survival of the population, false - otherwise). This is relative viability, taking into account its impact on the population.
H - Health: good physical and mental condition of the individual. In more detail:
H = (F ‚àß I ‚àß P)F - Physical health: no serious illnesses or injuries, sufficient physical strength and endurance.
I - Immunity: the body's ability to resist infections and diseases.
M - Mental health: stable emotional state, ability to cope with stress, no mental disorders.

A - Adaptability: the ability of an individual to adapt to changing environmental conditions. This is, at a minimum:
A = (O ‚àß H ‚àß O)O - Learning ability: the ability to acquire new knowledge and skills.
Skills: Possession of useful skills for survival and adaptation (e.g., obtaining food, building shelter, protecting oneself from predators).
Adaptability: Ability to respond effectively to new challenges and changes in the environment.

Threats: Presence of external threats to the individual (e.g., predators, disease, starvation, adverse weather conditions).
Coping Ability: Possession of means and abilities to protect oneself from threats and ensure one's own safety and, possibly, the safety of the group (impact on population survivability). In more detail:
Coping = (Zsh ‚à® Sk ‚à® Sv)Zsh - Defense: Possession of physical defense (e.g., robust constitution, claws, teeth, venom) or ability to use tools for defense.
Sk - Stealth: Ability to avoid threats, hide from predators, or find shelter.
Sv - Cooperative Ability: Ability to cooperate with other members of the population for joint protection or resource extraction. This point is critical for social species, including humans.

Interpretation of the formula:
(H ‚àß A): An individual must be both healthy and adaptive to be considered viable in the context of population survival. Health provides the physical ability to survive, while adaptability allows for adjustment to changing conditions. If one of these aspects is missing or insufficient, it affects the survival of the population as a whole.
(U ‚Üí Cp): If threats exist (U), then an individual must have the ability to resist them (Cp). If threats exist, but the individual is unable to defend itself, its viability decreases and, as a result, its contribution to the overall viability of the population.
Wo = ‚Ä¶: Viability of an individual (Wo) is defined as a combination of health, adaptability, and the ability to resist threats. If an individual is healthy and adaptive, and is able to resist threats (when they exist), then it is viable and can contribute to the survival of the population.
Additional notes:
This model reflects the relationship between individual viability and population survival.
The factor ‚ÄúInteraction Ability‚Äù is critically important for social species, since collective protection and cooperation significantly increase the chances of survival of both individuals and the entire population.
This model can be used to assess the risk of extinction of individual populations and develop measures for their conservation.
This model, although logical, requires numerical estimates of the parameters Z, A and Cp for practical application. This is a complex but necessary task for the analysis of specific populations.
In conclusion, the viability of an individual, as an element of a population, is determined by its health, adaptability and ability to withstand threats. These qualities, in turn, are the foundation for the survival of the entire population.



A logical formula for genetic balance for the eternal existence of a population.

Unfortunately, it is impossible to create a logical formula that guarantees the eternal existence of a population. The existence of any population in the real world depends on a huge number of interacting factors that cannot be perfectly modeled by logic.

However, we can formulate a logical expression that describes the necessary but not sufficient conditions for maintaining genetic balance, which contributes to the stability of the population. Here is an example, with explanations:

Notations:

G: The set of all genes in the population.
A(g): The set of alleles for gene g (where g belongs to G).
f(a): The frequency of allele a in the population (where a belongs to A(g)).
Œµ: The minimum acceptable value of the allele frequency (a small positive number to avoid complete extinction).
t: Time (discrete or continuous).
Œîf(a, t): Change in allele frequency a at time t.
Œº: Maximum allowable change in allele frequency per unit time (small positive number, to limit drift).
S(a): Selective advantage of allele a (if S(a) > 0, then the allele is favorable, if S(a) < 0, then it is unfavorable, if S(a) = 0, then it is neutral).
Œ∏: Maximum allowable value of absolute selective advantage (small positive number).
R: Set of all possible combinations of genes (genotypes).
E: Set of environmental factors.
Œ¶(R, E): Fitness function of a genotype from set R in a given environmental environment from set E.
Œ∑: Minimum allowable value of the fitness function.
M: Population size.
M_min: Minimum allowable population size.
Logical Formula (necessary conditions):

‚àÄg‚ààG ‚àÄa‚ààA(g) (
(f(a, t) > Œµ) ‚àß // The frequency of each allele must be above a minimum threshold
(|Œîf(a, t)| < Œº) ‚àß // The change in the frequency of each allele must be limited
(|S(a)| < Œ∏) // The absolute selective advantage must be limited
) ‚àß
‚àÄr‚ààR ‚àÄe‚ààE (Œ¶(r,e) > Œ∑) ‚àß // The fitness function for any genotype and any environment must be above a minimum threshold
(M > M_min) // The population size must be greater than the minimum value
Explanation:
First part (‚àÄg‚ààG ‚àÄa‚ààA(g) ‚Ä¶): For each gene g and each allele a of this gene, the following conditions must be satisfied: f(a, t) > Œµ: The frequency of allele a at time t must be greater than Œµ. This prevents alleles from disappearing completely from the population.
|Œîf(a, t)| < Œº: The absolute value of the change in frequency of allele a at time t must be less than Œº. This limits genetic drift and abrupt fluctuations that can lead to loss of genetic diversity.
|S(a)| < Œ∏: The absolute value of the selective advantage of allele a must be less than Œ∏. This condition limits strong selection in either direction, which can reduce diversity.

The second part (‚àÄr‚ààR ‚àÄe‚ààE (Œ¶(r,e) > Œ∑)): For each genotype r and each environmental condition e, the fitness function Œ¶(r, e) must be greater than Œ∑. This means that each genotype must be able to survive and reproduce in every possible ecological setting.
Third part (M > M_min): The population size (M) must be greater than the minimum size (M_min) required to maintain genetic diversity and avoid inbreeding.
Important notes:
Insufficiency: This formula represents necessary but insufficient conditions. Even if all these conditions are met, this does not guarantee that the population will survive forever. Unforeseen events, mutations, new diseases, drastic environmental changes, and other factors can lead to extinction.
Idealization: This formula is a strong simplification of reality. Real interactions between genes and the environment are extremely complex and cannot be fully described by logic.
Parameter dependence: The values ‚Äã‚Äãof the parameters Œµ, Œº, Œ∏, Œ∑, and M_min are critical and depend on the specific population and its environment. Determining these values ‚Äã‚Äãis a complex task.
Time Factor: The formula refers to a time t. For eternal existence, these conditions must be met for all times t in the future, which is impossible to predict.
No Mutation Accounting: The formula in its current form implicitly assumes that mutations are either absent or that their effect on allele frequency is negligible. In reality, mutations are a source of new genetic diversity, but can also be harmful.

In conclusion:

While it is impossible to construct a logical formula for ‚Äúeternal existence‚Äù, this formula provides a basis for understanding and modeling the factors that influence genetic balance and population stability. However, one should be aware of the limitations and simplifications inherent in any such model. Real biological existence is a complex and dynamic system that defies complete logical description.



The minimum percentage of genetic diversity in a population is a concept that depends on many factors and cannot be defined unambiguously. There is no universal value that would guarantee the survival and prosperity of any population. However, it is possible to identify key concepts and factors that influence the definition of this minimum, as well as to give a general idea of ‚Äã‚Äãthe recommended values. Human.

Key concepts and factors:

Effective population size (Ne): This is the most important factor. It represents the number of individuals that contribute to the next generation (reproduce effectively). Usually, the effective population size is much smaller than the actual (total) population size. The smaller Ne, the faster the loss of genetic diversity due to genetic drift and inbreeding.

Inbreeding Rate: Inbreeding (crossing closely related individuals) results in increased homozygosity (having the same alleles at a locus), which can lead to the expression of recessive deleterious alleles, decreased fertility, disease resistance, and other problems that reduce fitness.

Mutation Rate: Mutations are a source of new genetic diversity. A high mutation rate can compensate for the loss of diversity, but also increases the likelihood of deleterious mutations.

Selective Pressure: If environmental conditions are stable and there is no strong selection, a population can maintain a lower level of genetic diversity. However, if conditions change (disease, climate change, etc.), populations with higher genetic diversity have a better chance of adapting.

Population History: Population bottlenecks (reduction of population size to very few individuals) and other demographic events in the past can significantly reduce genetic diversity.

Type of organism: Species with a faster life cycle, high fecundity, and larger population size may support higher genetic diversity than species with a slow life cycle, low fecundity, and small population size.

Loci and alleles: Genetic diversity can be measured in different ways: Observed heterozygosity (Ho): The percentage of individuals in a population that are heterozygous (having two different alleles at a given locus).
Expected heterozygosity (He): The probability that two randomly selected alleles in a population will be different (also known as Shannon-Weaver diversity).
Alleles per locus (A): The more alleles at a locus, the greater the genetic diversity.
Microsatellite or SNP diversity: More modern methods that allow diversity to be assessed at the genome-wide level.

Recommendations and general ideas:
The ‚Äú50/500‚Äù rule: Traditionally, the ‚Äú50/500‚Äù rule has been proposed. Ne (the effective population size) should be at least 50 to avoid the short-term effects of inbreeding. Ne should be at least 500 to maintain genetic diversity and long-term adaptation to environmental change. However, this rule is greatly simplified and may not be appropriate for all species.
Minimum Ne: Many experts now believe that Ne should be much greater than 500 for many species, especially those susceptible to environmental change. Recommended values ‚Äã‚Äãoften range from a few hundred to a few thousand. Ne of 5000 and above provides more robust genetic diversity, especially in changing environments.
Heterozygosity level: There is no universal minimum heterozygosity. However, if the observed heterozygosity (Ho) falls below 0.1, it may indicate low genetic diversity and an increased risk of inbreeding. It is important to compare with other populations of the same species to assess whether the heterozygosity level is low for the species. Expected heterozygosity (He) is often a more useful metric because it takes into account allele frequencies. The goal is to maintain He at a level close to that seen in healthy, unstressed populations of the same species.
Risk assessment: When managing populations, especially those that are endangered, genetic analysis should be performed to assess the level of genetic diversity and the risk of inbreeding. It is also important to consider environmental factors and population history.

Conclusion:
Determining the minimum percentage of genetic diversity requires a comprehensive approach that takes into account many factors. There is no simple answer that would be suitable for all cases. Recommended values ‚Äã‚Äãfor effective population size (Ne), heterozygosity, and other indicators depend on the specific species, its life cycle, history, and environmental conditions. The key principle is to strive to maintain genetic diversity comparable to that observed in healthy, unthreatened populations of the same species. In general, the higher the genetic diversity, the greater the chances of a population to survive and adapt to changing conditions.

Hierarchy in the species by importance for the continuation of the species. Logical model, logical proof and logical formula.

Hierarchy by importance for the continuation of the species (logical model, proof and formula)
I propose a hierarchy built on the basis of the importance of factors for the survival of the species, presented in a logical model, proof and formula.

1. Hierarchy (from least to most important for the continuation of the species):

Individual survival: The ability of an individual to survive in the environment (find food, avoid predators, cope with diseases). This is the foundation, but not a guarantee of the continuation of the species.

Reproduction: The ability to produce offspring. A necessary but not sufficient condition. The quantity and quality of offspring are important.

Social structure (if applicable): Interaction within a population, cooperation, division of labor (e.g. hunting, defense), care for offspring. Increases efficiency, but is not necessary for survival.

Adaptation to changing conditions: The ability of a species to adjust to changes in the environment (climate, availability of resources, emergence of new threats). Key factor for long-term survival. Includes genetic diversity, behavioral flexibility, and the ability to evolve.

Availability of necessary resources: Availability of food, water, suitable habitat. Without these, survival is impossible, even if the species is well adapted and reproduces.

Defense against external threats: The ability of a species to avoid or resist threats such as predators, diseases, natural disasters, competition with other species, human activity. Crucial for the continuation of the species.

2. Logical model:

Let's represent the factors as logical variables:

I: Individual survival (True - the individual survives, False - the individual dies).
R: Reproduction (True - reproduces, False - does not reproduce).
S: Social structure (True - present, False - absent/ineffective).
A: Adaptation (True - adapted, False - not adapted/poorly adapted).
E: Availability of necessary resources (True - present, False - not).
P: Protection from external threats (True - protected, False - unprotected).
V: Continuation of the species' existence (True - the species survives, False - the species dies out).
3. Logical proof and logical formula:

Let's assume that the following are necessary for the species to survive:

Availability of resources (E)
Protection from threats (P)
Adaptability (A)
Reproduction (R)
Other factors increase the chances, but are not critical.

Logical proof:

If there are no resources (¬¨E), the species dies out (¬¨V).
If there is no protection (¬¨P), the species dies out (¬¨V).
If there is no adaptation (¬¨A), the species dies out (over time, ¬¨V).
If there is no reproduction (¬¨R), the species dies out (¬¨V).
If there are resources (E) AND there is protection (P) AND there is adaptation (A) AND there is reproduction (R), then the species survives (V). This is the basic condition.
Logical formula:

V ‚â° (E ‚àß P ‚àß A ‚àß R) ‚à® (E ‚àß P ‚àß A ‚àß R ‚àß S) ‚à® (E ‚àß P ‚àß A ‚àß R ‚àß S ‚àß I)

Where:

‚â° means ‚Äúis equivalent to‚Äù (that is, V is true if and only if the condition on the right is true).
‚àß means ‚ÄúAND‚Äù (logical multiplication).
‚à® means ‚ÄúOR‚Äù (logical addition).
¬¨ means ‚ÄúNOT‚Äù (negation).
Interpretation of the formula:

The formula states that a species survives (V) if:

It has the necessary resources (E), is protected from threats (P), adapts (A), and reproduces (R) (base case).
OR, in addition to the base case, it has a social structure (S) - which increases the chances.
OR, in addition to the base case and social structure, individuals survive (I) - which further increases the chances.
Conclusion:

This model is a simplified representation of a complex system. In reality, many factors are interconnected and influence each other. For example, social structure can improve adaptation, and adaptation can affect the need for resources. However, the proposed hierarchy and logical model allow us to formalize the idea of ‚Äã‚Äãthe key factors influencing the survival of a species.

Let's add intelligence/ability and genetic traits to our model, taking into account their impact on the survival of the species.

1. Updated hierarchy:

Let's add two new elements, arranging them logically in the context of the previous hierarchy:

Individual survival
Reproduction
Social structure
Intelligence and abilities: Cognitive and behavioral characteristics of individuals that allow them to solve complex problems, learn, adapt, and use resources more efficiently. This may include both innate abilities and acquired skills.
Adaptation to changing conditions
Availability of necessary resources
Genetic features (key individuals): The presence in the population of individuals with unique genetic combinations that give them advantages in certain conditions (e.g. resistance to diseases, increased fertility, more efficient use of resources). It is important to note that this is not all genetic diversity, but namely key features.
Protection from external threats
2. Updated logical model:

Adding new logical variables:

Q: Intelligence and abilities (True - sufficient/high level, False - low/insufficient level). Estimated at the population level or average value.
G: Genetic traits (key individuals) (True - present, False - absent).

3. Updated logical proof and logical formula:

The formula will now include new factors. We will change the weight of some of the old factors, since intelligence and genetic traits can influence adaptation. For example, a species may adapt more quickly to climate change if individuals have sufficient intelligence to change their behavior or exploit new resources, or if the population includes individuals with genetic resistance to new diseases arising from climate change.

We assume that intelligence, adaptation, and genetic traits work together to ensure long-term survival.

Logical proof (updated):

If there are no resources (¬¨E), the species goes extinct (¬¨V).
If there are no defenses (¬¨P), the species goes extinct (¬¨V).
If there is no adaptation (¬¨A), and no intelligence (¬¨Q), and no key genetic traits (¬¨G), the species goes extinct (¬¨V). These factors are interrelated. If the species as a whole is not adapted, and there is no intelligence to compensate for this, and there are no ‚Äúsaving‚Äù genetic variants, then extinction is very likely.
If there is no reproduction (¬¨R), the species goes extinct (¬¨V).
If there are resources (E) AND there is protection (P) AND (there is adaptation (A) OR there is intelligence (Q) OR there are key genetic traits (G)) AND there is reproduction (R), then the species survives (V). This is the basic condition.
Logical formula (updated):

V ‚â° (E ‚àß P ‚àß (A ‚à® (Q ‚àß G)) ‚àß R) ‚à® (E ‚àß P ‚àß (A ‚à® (Q ‚àß G)) ‚àß R ‚àß S) ‚à® (E ‚àß P ‚àß (A ‚à® (Q ‚àß G)) ‚àß R ‚àß S ‚àß I)

Where:

‚à® means ‚ÄúOR‚Äù (logical addition). The important thing is that adaptation (A) is key, but intelligence (Q) and key genetic features (G) can compensate for it by working together
‚àß means ‚ÄúAND‚Äù (logical multiplication).
Interpretation of the updated formula:

A species survives if it satisfies the following conditions:

Resources and Protection: There is access to the necessary resources (E) and sufficient protection from external threats (P). These are basic needs.

Adaptation OR Intelligence and Genetics: A species must either be sufficiently adapted to the environment (A) or have both sufficient intelligence (Q) and the presence of key genetic traits (G) in the population. It is important to understand that intelligence and genetic traits work together. High intelligence without genetic variability will not help if, for example, a new disease comes along. Conversely, genetic resistance to disease without intelligence will not optimize resource use.

Reproduction: A species must reproduce successfully (R).

Additional factors: The presence of social structure (S) and a high level of individual survival (I) further increase the chances of survival.

Additional notes:

Factor dependency: This model emphasizes the interdependence of factors. For example, higher intelligence can lead to better adaptation and more efficient use of resources. Genetic traits can influence disease resistance and the ability to adapt to new conditions.

Key individuals: It is important to emphasize that we are not talking about the entire genetic diversity of a population, but about key genetic traits that provide a significant advantage in specific conditions.

Simplification: This model is still a simplification of the real world. In reality, a huge number of factors influence the survival of a species.



It is unacceptable for a system to exterminate members of a population and species on which the continued existence of an individual species and the entire population depends.



What special genetic traits give the ability to rapidly restore physiological and conscious states. Logical model, logical proof, and logical formula. Mermaid diagram.

The question of the genetic traits that give the ability to rapidly restore physiological and conscious states is very complex and does not yet have a clear answer. There is no ‚Äúone gene‚Äù responsible for this. It is likely the result of a complex interaction of many genes and environmental factors. However, we can hypothesize some genetic factors that could potentially play a role and build a logical model based on current scientific knowledge.

Potential Genetic Factors:

Genes involved in immune system regulation: A more effective immune system can more quickly deal with inflammation and tissue damage, promoting faster physical recovery. Genes that control cytokines (eg, IL-10, TGF-Œ≤), immune cell receptors (eg, TLR), and MHC (major histocompatibility complex) may be associated with faster recovery.
Genes involved in the regulation of tissue regeneration and repair processes: Some people may have a genetically determined higher capacity for tissue regeneration (eg, muscle after injury, nerve tissue after minor trauma). Genes encoding growth factors (eg, BDNF, NGF, IGF-1), extracellular matrix proteins, and enzymes involved in tissue remodeling (eg, metalloproteinases) may be relevant.
Genes involved in the regulation of neuroplasticity and neurogenesis: Neuroplasticity is the brain's ability to adapt and change in response to experience. Higher neuroplasticity may allow the brain to recover more quickly from stress, injury, or changes in consciousness. Neurogenesis is the process of generating new neurons. Genes that regulate BDNF, synaptogenesis, and neuronal differentiation may play a role.
Genes involved in the regulation of the stress response (HPA axis): A more effective and balanced stress response may prevent prolonged exposure to cortisol and other stress hormones that can slow recovery. Genes encoding glucocorticoid receptors (e.g., NR3C1), enzymes involved in cortisol synthesis, and proteins that regulate the HPA axis may be associated with faster recovery.
Genes involved in metabolism and energy metabolism: Efficient metabolism and the ability to quickly replenish energy stores may contribute to faster recovery, both physical and mental. Genes involved in glycolysis, mitochondrial function, and glucose transport may be relevant.
Genes involved in sleep regulation: The quality and duration of sleep are critical for recovery. Genes that control circadian rhythms (e.g., PER3, CLOCK), melatonin production (e.g., ASMT), and sleep architecture may influence the ability to recover quickly.
Logical Model:

Premise 1: Rapid recovery of physiological and conscious states is the result of coordinated work of various biological systems (immune, nervous, endocrine, metabolic).

Premise 2: The functioning of these systems is partly determined by genetic factors.

Premise 3: Certain genetic variants (alleles) in genes involved in the regulation of these systems may provide for their more efficient functioning and, therefore, faster recovery.

Conclusion: Individuals with favorable genetic variants in genes regulating the immune system, tissue regeneration, neuroplasticity, stress response, metabolism, and sleep have a genetic predisposition to faster recovery of physiological and conscious states.

Logical Proof (Hypothetical):

Observation: Elite athletes show differences in recovery rates after intense exercise.
Hypothesis: These differences are partly due to genetic factors.
Experiment: Comparison of the genomes of athletes with fast and slow recovery.
Results (Hypothetical): Statistically significant differences were found in the frequencies of certain alleles in genes related to the immune system (e.g. IL-10), muscle regeneration (e.g. IGF-1), and neuroplasticity (e.g. BDNF).
Conclusion: The presence of certain genetic variants correlates with faster recovery after exercise, supporting the hypothesis of a genetic predisposition.
Logical Formula (Simplified):

R = f(G1, G2, G3, ..., Gn, E)

Where:

R = Recovery rate
G1, G2, G3, ..., Gn = Genetic factors (alleles of genes related to immunity, regeneration, neuroplasticity, stress, metabolism, sleep)
E = Environmental factors (nutrition, sleep, exercise, stress level, etc.)
f = Function describing the interaction of genetic and environmental factors that determine the rate of recovery.

Important Notes:
This model is a simplification of a complex biological reality.
The interactions between genes and the environment are extremely complex and not yet fully understood.
The identified ‚Äúcandidate genes‚Äù may have a small effect, and their effect may vary greatly depending on genetic background and environmental factors.
Further research is needed to confirm these hypotheses and identify specific genetic variants that promote rapid recovery.

The influence of epigenetic factors (changes in gene expression that are not related to changes in DNA sequence) may also be significant.
In conclusion, rapid recovery ability is likely a polygenic trait influenced by environmental factors. The presented logic model and diagram reflect the current understanding of this complex relationship, highlighting the need for further research.

If your successes are false, then your successes cannot be the foundation of your Victory. Logical model, logical proof, and logical formula.

Logical model:
Success: Some achievement, a positive result.
False success: Success based on incorrect information, deception, chance, incompetence, or other factors that make success unstable or unfounded.
Victory: The final, stable, significant result achieved as a result of struggle, competition, or solving a difficult problem.
Foundation: The basis on which something is built. In this context, the basis for achieving Victory.
Logical proof:
Goal: To prove that false successes cannot be the foundation of Victory.

Arguments:

Axiom 1: The foundation must be strong and reliable. It must withstand loads and remain stable.
Axiom 2: False success is inherently unreliable and unstable. It may disappear, be exposed, or fail to withstand subsequent testing.
Argument 1 (by contradiction): Assume that false success can be the foundation of Victory. This means that Victory built on a false foundation will be stable and durable.
Contradiction: However, Victory built on false success will be vulnerable to exposure, changing circumstances, or lack of genuine knowledge and skills. Eventually, the false foundation will collapse and the Victory will be lost.
Conclusion: The assumption is false. Therefore, false successes cannot be the foundation of Victory.
Additional considerations:

False successes can create the illusion of progress, which leads to complacency and an unwillingness to make real efforts to achieve sustainable results.
False successes can lead to making wrong decisions in the future, based on erroneous information.
Ultimately, only honest, well-founded achievements can serve as a solid foundation for a real Victory.
Logical formula:
Let's denote:

U: Success
L: Falsehood (an operator that applies to success)
P: Victory
F(x, y): Foundation (where x is the foundation, y is what is being built)
Then we can formulate:

L(U): False success.
F(L(U), P) = False: False success cannot be the foundation of Victory.
Or, more formally:

¬¨ ‚àÉ x: F(L(U), P) where x ‚àà {F(x, P) | x is the foundation, P is Victory}
This formula says that there is no case where false success is the foundation of Victory.



Not all, not everything, and not always. Logical model, logical proof, and logical formula.

Logical model:
All: Covers absolutely all elements of a certain set.
All: Covers all objects, aspects, or circumstances within the situation under consideration.
Always: Covers all moments in time, continuously and without exception.
Not all: There are elements of the set for which the statement is not true.
Not all: There are objects, aspects, or circumstances for which the statement is not true.
Not always: There are moments in time for which the statement is not true.
Proposition (P): A statement whose truth is questioned.
Logical proof:
Goal: Prove that the statement ‚ÄúAll, everything, and always‚Äù is not always true. That is, show that ‚ÄúNot all, not everything, and not always‚Äù is a general reality.

Method: Proof through counterexamples. We will show that there is a situation when ‚Äúall,‚Äù ‚Äúeverything,‚Äù and ‚Äúalways‚Äù are not fulfilled simultaneously.

Arguments:

‚ÄúAll‚Äù: Consider the statement, ‚ÄúAll people like a certain type of food (e.g. pizza).‚Äù This is false because there are people who do not like pizza. Therefore, ‚Äúnot all.‚Äù

‚ÄúAll‚Äù: Consider the statement, ‚ÄúEverything a person does is good.‚Äù This is false because people make mistakes, hurt others, and do bad things. Therefore, ‚Äúnot all.‚Äù

‚ÄúAlways‚Äù: Consider the statement, ‚ÄúThe sun always shines.‚Äù This is false because the sun does not shine at night, nor does it shine on cloudy days. Therefore, ‚Äúnot always.‚Äù

Pairing: Suppose a statement is, ‚ÄúAll students always like all classes at university.‚Äù This is obviously false. There are students who do not like some classes, and even those who like a class in general are not always enthusiastic about every topic or class. This violates ‚Äúall,‚Äù ‚Äúall,‚Äù and ‚Äúalways‚Äù at the same time.

Conclusion: Since there are counterexamples to ‚Äúall‚Äù, ‚Äúeverything‚Äù, and ‚Äúalways‚Äù, a statement that covers them all is not always true. Therefore, ‚ÄúNot all, not everything, and not always‚Äù is a reflection of reality. In other words, generalizations that claim to be absolutely true are often false.

Logical formula:
Let:

P(x): A statement about an element x.
A: A set of elements to which the statement applies.
T: Moments of time.
O: Objects, aspects, or circumstances.
Then:

‚ÄúEveryone, everything, and always‚Äù: ‚àÄx ‚àà A, ‚àÄo ‚àà O, ‚àÄt ‚àà T: P(x, o, t) (For all x in A, for all o in O, for all t in T, the statement P is true.)

‚ÄúNot everyone, not everything, and not always‚Äù: ¬¨(‚àÄx ‚àà A, ‚àÄo ‚àà O, ‚àÄt ‚àà T: P(x, o, t)) which is equivalent to: ‚àÉx ‚àà A, ‚àÉo ‚àà O, ‚àÉt ‚àà T: ¬¨P(x, o, t) (There is x in A, there is o in O, there is t in T for which the statement P is not true.)

The formula shows that the negation of the statement ‚ÄúEveryone, everything, and always‚Äù means that it is enough to find at least one an element, object, or point in time for which a statement fails to hold, in order to prove its falsity.
